  :                            121 				. the big banks descriptor stack
  :                            122 				. has space for a potential two-word
  :                            123 				. descriptor for each 262144-word bank

  :                            127 				. system memory in IO-mapped

  :                            136 				. LS 6 bits in the relocation

  :                            139 				. dual ported network I/F memories

  :                            142 
  :                            143 				. however, when BIG/BANK is off,
  :                            144 				. the six low bits select a page
  :                            145 				. within the system memory bank

  :                            148 
  :                            149

  :                            284 				. upwards and compresses downwards, and
  :                            285 				. the stack of big banks, which grows
  :                            286 				. downwards and compresses upwards

  :                            290 				. one to nearly 65536 adjacent physical
  :                            291 				. banks of 262144 words

  :                            293 				. A single large bank may contain nearly
  :                            294 				. 1024 times the 16 Megaword Address space

  :                            296 				. for large arrays which need to be contiguous
  :                            297 
  :                            298 				. An array needs to be contiguous if

  :                            301 
  :                            302 				. Developers can also structure arrays

  :                            304 				. are safely inside one bank, even if
  :                            305 				. the containing structure straddles two
  :                            306 				. non-contiguous banks addressed with
  :                            307 				. two adjacent relocation base-registers
  :                            308 
  :                            309 				. Operand EA is translated to an absolute
  :                            310 				. address once for each operand read or write
  :                            311 
  :                            312 				. However very large contiguous bank
  :                            313 				. arrays are obtained with one ARRAY$ call

  :                            318 				. a time. Structures straddling bank boundaries
  :                            319 				. are accessed using two adjacent base registers

  :                            282 .	each handle integer corresponds to a 48-bit encoding
  :                            283 .	starting at handle(1)
  :                            284 
  :                            285 .	the index corresponds to a port number or to a
  :                            286 .	TCP connection and is the encoded offset of the
  :                            287 .	control block in the containing bank
  :                            288

  :                            226 .	bank

  :                            39 
  :                            40 . Emulation implements one kind of network interface
  :                            41 
  :                            42 . this can be modified when there is more than one interface type
  :                            43 . initially the only known type is modelled as a dual ported memory
  :                            44 . interface to the system
  :                            45 .
  :                            46 . half of the interfacing memory presents input frames
  :                            47 . the other half is available for output frames
  :                            48 
  :                            49 
  :                            50 .		_________________________________
  :                            51 .	>------>|1st receive buffer		|
  :                            52 .	|	|2nd				|
  :                            53 .	|	|				|
  :                            54 .	^
  :                            55 .	|
  :                            56 .	<-------|last receive buffer		|
  :                            57 .		|_______________________________|
  :                            58 .	>------>|1st transmit buffer		|
  :                            59 .	|	|2nd				|
  :                            60 .	|	|				|
  :                            61 .	^
  :                            62 .	|
  :                            63 .	<-------|last transmit buffer		|
  :                            64 .		|_______________________________|
  :                            65 
  :                            66 
  :                            67 . the frames arrive and are sent in cyclic order of the buffers
  :                            68 .
  :                            69 . there is an 8-word length + ethernet header at the start of each buffer
  :                            70 . this sketch represents a quartet of bits per symbol
  :                            71 
  :                            72 .	 00LLLL 00xxxx 00xxxx 00xxxx 00yyyy 00yyyy 00yyyy 00pppp
  :                            73 
  :                            74 .	________________________________________________________________________
  :                            75 .	| 00LLLL | 00xxxx | 00xxxx | 00xxxx | 00yyyy | 00yyyy | 00yyyy | 00pppp|
  :                            76 .	|________|________|________|________|________|________|________|_______|
  :                            77 .	| 00data | 00....
  :                            78 
  :                            79 
  :                            80 . network data is buffered 16 bits per computer word right up to the socket
  :                            81 . and XDR interfaces. These "interface dual ported" memories may be 16-bit
  :                            82 . memories reading out eight zero bits at the high order positions of each
  :                            83 . computer word, or they may be 24-bit memories: then the high-order 8 bits
  :                            84 . must be maintained with zero values
  :                            85 
  :                            86 .	LLLL	is frame size, and nonzero value written here indicates
  :                            87 .		a complete frame received, or available for transmission
  :                            88 .
  :                            89 .	xxxx xxxx xxxx	is the destination ethernet address
  :                            90 
  :                            91 .	yyyy yyyy yyyy	is the source ethernet address
  :                            92 
  :                            93 .	pppp	is the protocol family indicator, 0800 for IPv4, 0806 for ARP
  :                            94 .		as many as required
  :                            95 
  :                            96 
  :                            97 . the buffers are equal sized and used cyclically
  :                            98 
  :                            99 . the input machine signals to software that a frame is available
  :                            100 . by writing a length in bits 15:0 of the first word in the buffer:
  :                            101 .
  :                            102 .		0000 0000 LLLL LLLL LLLL LLLL
  :                            103 .
  :                            104 . The length is not self-inclusive. It is the frame length.
  :                            105 . The frame starts at the next word
  :                            106 
  :                            107 . software indicates that the frame has been sampled and the buffer is
  :                            108 . free by writing zero to this length word
  :                            109 
  :                            110 . software indicates that a frame for transmission is assembled 
  :                            111 . in the transmission buffer by writing a length in bits 15:0 of
  :                            112 . the first word in the transmission buffer. The length is the
  :                            113 . frame length and not self-inclusive
  :                            114 
  :                            115 . The frame for transmission starts in the second word of the
  :                            116 . transmission buffer
  :                            117 
  :                            118 . Transmission completion is indicated back to the software by zero
  :                            119 . in the length word, and this also indicates the transmission buffer
  :                            120 . is again free
  :                            121 
  :                            122 . This interface model is not part of RTA architecture but supports
  :                            123 . this emulation and is intended to work like network interfaces are 
  :                            124 . intended for RTA systems
  :                            125 . 
  :                            126 . That is, some of the 63 DEVICEs (arrays outside system memory)
  :                            127 . should carry network data to and from an RTA system.
  :                            128 
  :                            129 . A DEVICE is up to 65536 BANKS of up to 262144 WORDS of up to 24 bits
  :                            130 .
  :                            131 
  :                            132 . device 0 is system memory or executable space and is up to 16 gigawords
  :                            133 . of 24-bit memory
  :                            134 
  :                            135 . only system memory is selectable in pages of 4096 words as well as in
  :                            136 . banks of 256K
  :                            137 
  :                            138 . Memory in the other 63 possible devices is selectable in banks of
  :                            139 . 262144 words only
  :                            140 
  :                            141 . the pointer to a page of system memory based in one of the 72 relocation
  :                            142 . registers has the layout
  :                            143 
  :                            144 .	00pp pppp pppp pppp pppp pppp
  :                            145 
  :                            146 . where bit 22 = 0 indicates a 22-bit page pointer and the task has a granule
  :                            147 . of 4096 words addressed and available
  :                            148 
  :                            149 . the pointer to a bank of system memory in a relocation register has the layout
  :                            150 
  :                            151 .	01pp pppp pppp pppp pp00 0000
  :                            152 
  :                            153 . where bit 22 = 1 indicates a 16-bit bank pointer and the task has a granule
  :                            154 . of 262144 words addressed and available
  :                            155 
  :                            156 . bits 5:0 = 00 0000 indicate that the bank is in system memory
  :                            157 .
  :                            158 . the pointer to a bank of "I/O Memory" (strictly an array other than
  :                            159 . system memory) has the layout in a relocation register
  :                            160 
  :                            161 .	01bb bbbb bbbb bbbb bbDE VICE
  :                            162 
  :                            163 . where bit 22 = 1 = Big Bank ** DEVICE = nonzero = some other device than
  :                            164 . system memory
  :                            165 
  :                            166 . Some of these 63 possible arrays outside system memory will be battery-
  :                            167 . supported SRAMS of 24-bit words containing realtime transaction file systems
  :                            168 
  :                            169 . Others may be flash memories containing default system images and occasional
  :                            170 . data backups (but flash memories may not be used for high-frequency
  :                            171 . transaction update data)
  :                            172 
  :                            173 . some may be insertable flashes containing 16- or 8-bit memories organised
  :                            174 . in a file system architecture readable and writable by other systems
  :                            175 
  :                            176 . some may be video RAMs of up to 16 Gigawords of 24-bit words
  :                            177 
  :                            178 . At least one device must be an array interfacing a network adaptor to the
  :                            179 . RTA system with 16 network data bits per computer word as modelled here
  :                            180 
  :                            181 . This interface array is supplied to the emulator by a Unix shared memory
  :                            182 . block of 524288 bytes which looks to RTA like a device of one bank of
  :                            183 . 262144 16-bit words.
  :                            184 
  :                            185 . RTA reads each word out as a 24-bit computer word with bits 23:16 = 0000 0000
  :                            186 . The first half of this block is receive buffers and the second half transmit
  :                            187 . buffers
  :                            188 
  :                            189 . the settings here are that RPAGEW is 1024 words. This is the buffer size
  :                            190 . and it allows an ethernet frame of 1514+ bytes. Jumbos are not accounted
  :                            191 . for in the emulation. A device in a large-configuration RTA may have
  :                            192 . 65536 * 262144 words in a network interface device, but the emulator has
  :                            193 . only 1 * 262144 words in its one network interface space.
  :                            194 
  :                            195 . Therefore the emulator will only test standard ethernet traffic
  :                            196 
  :                            197 . the configuration of the emulated interface (which links to a real interface
  :                            198 . outside the emulator) is therefore
  :                            199 
  :                            200 .	128 receive buffers of 1024 words each
  :                            201 . plus	128 transmit buffers of 1024 words each
  :                            202 
  :                            203 
  :                            204 . Notes. Note 1
  :                            205 
  :                            206 . To avoid accidental damage, device banks (as distinct from system memory
  :                            207 . banks) may only be based by interrupt code. This avoids inadvertant
  :                            208 . traversal of array accesses across all the devices. At its simplest,
  :                            209 . the internal interrupts (system calls II 64+32 .. II 64+63) only need
  :                            210 . to copy the bank handle from user-A register, base it, and return:
  :                            211 
  :                            212 .		la	A
  :                            213 .		sabr	32
  :                            214 .		ir	0
  :                            215 
  :                            216 		.
  :                            217 		.
  :                            218 
  :                            219 .		la	A
  :                            220 .		sabr	63
  :                            221 .		ir	0
  :                            222 
  :                            223 . but as much extra security can be added to that as wished in more complex OS
  :                            224 
  :                            225 . Once the storage area is based, the application can access it at will.
  :                            226 . If this is not wished, the interrupt code itself must carry out required
  :                            227 . operations and unbase the storage area before return. The simplest model
  :                            228 . here relies on trusted applications which base the sensitive banks
  :                            229 . by design and not by accident
  :                            230 
  :                            231 . This also provides the beginning of a security model for any implementation
  :                            232 . which includes the translatable storage handle
  :                            233 
  :                            234 . Where a handle 0xxx xxxx xxxx xxxx xxxx is absolute whether it points
  :                            235 . to system memory or a device
  :                            236 
  :                            237 . 1xxx xxxx xxxx xxxx xxxx is reserved for a possible translating range of
  :                            238 . storage area handles, for moveable/swappable application storage.
  :                            239 . A hardware implementation which does translatable handles would reserve
  :                            240 . the basing of all absolute areas (device and system memory) to privileged
  :                            241 . code of some sort, perhaps system calls as in this initial model
  :                            242 
  :                            243 . applications could then only directly base an allowed set of areas described
  :                            244 . in their translate lists.
  :                            245 
  :                            246 
  :                            247 
  :                            248 . Note 2
  :                            249 
  :                            250 . the interface handler here will offer each frame to the protocol family
  :                            251 . (mostly IPv4 or similar), which must decide immediately whether to take
  :                            252 . the frame and place it in storage visible to the application.
  :                            253 
  :                            254 . This means IP will check IP correctness, and call ICMP, UDP or TCP which
  :                            255 . must checksum as necessary while copying the datagram to socket storage.
  :                            256 
  :                            257 . This is not a flat-address system and there is no zero-copy
  :                            258 . receive. But there is only one copy, and it is carried out during checksum
  :                            259 . generation. After that the datagrams are in socket-associated buffers which
  :                            260 . are placed in the address space of the application.
  :                            261 
  :                            262 . The socket or XDR interface will format the data when the application
  :                            263 . subsequently receives it over the socket API.
  :                            264 
  :                            265 . on return from the call to the protocol family, the interface buffer is
  :                            266 . freed
  :                            267 
  :                            268 
  :                            269 
  :                            270 RX_LL_HL $equ	d$save
  :                            271 
  :                            272 
  :                            273 
  :                            274 $(1)
  :                            275 
  :                            276 .	pointer array to the network interface descriptors
  :                            277 .	these are numbered starting from one as in MIB-II
  :                            278 .	interfaces table
  :                            279 
  :                            280 .	word zero of this table is a count of the number of
  :                            281 .	interface descriptors in use
  :                            282 
  :                            283 .	the initial value 1 indicates that there is one
  :                            284 .	interface so far, interface(1) = internal loopback,
  :                            285 .	and the next to be configured ought to be interface(2)
  :                            286 
  :                            287 .	to obtain a descriptor for a new interface, increment
  :                            288 .	the counter in the first word of the array and select
  :                            289 .	the pointer at
  :                            290 
  :                            291 .	i_f_pointer_array[i_f_pointer_array[0]]
  :                            292 
  :                            293 .	for example
  :                            294 .
  :                            295 .		lx		i_f_pointer_array
  :                            296 .		ax, xi		1
  :                            297 .		sx		i_f_pointer_array
  :                            298 .		lx		0, x
  :                            299

  :                            41 HEADER_LONGWORDS $equ	d$save1	. 17 used in receive
  :                            42 TP_HEADER	$equ	d$save2	. 18 used in receive and transmit
  :                            43 I_F_IDX		$equ	d$save3	. 19 used in receive
  :                            44 
  :                            45 TX_AXW		$equ	8	. used in transmit
  :                            46 TX_FRAME	$equ	9	. used in receive and transmit
  :                            47 
  :                            48 HEADER_BYTES	$equ	d$save	. 16 used in transmit
  :                            49 MTU		$equ	d$save1	. 17 used in transmit
  :                            50 I_F		$equ	d$save2	. 19 used in transmit
  :                            51 
  :                            52 TX_FLAG_OFFSET	$equ	10	. used in tranmsit
  :                            53 TX_DATAGRAML	$equ	11	. used in transmit = unfragmented datagram
  :                            54 				. generated in interfaces = xmitted frame
  :                            55 
  :                            56 DYNAMIC_IP_HEADER $equ	0
  :                            57 		. if IP header length cannot be known at socket layer
  :                            58 		. then make this a multiple of 4 > 20 and < 64
  :                            59 		. to give a fixed large space for IP to add header
  :                            60 		. options
  :                            61 
  :                            62 		. otherwise IP header size is fixed when the socket
  :                            63 		. layer acquires a buffer

  :                            302 
  :                            303 .	ipv4_rx, input driver for a system with fixed dual ported
  :                            304 .	interface buffers
  :                            305 
  :                            306 .	this is an upward call from routine frames
  :                            307 
  :                            308 .	copying to socket-visible buffers is combined with
  :                            309 .	checksumming
  :                            310 
  :                            311 .	each word in the interface buffers is read only once
  :                            312 
  :                            313 .	ipv4_rx pushes and checks the IP headers in the
  :                            314 .	internal stack, which is a register array
  :                            315 
  :                            316 .	upper protocols obtain a buffer, then pop the IP headers
  :                            317 .	into it ahead of their own protocol message for
  :                            318 .	checksumming during transfer
  :                            319 
  :                            320 
00:00220E 50000F              +321 ipv4_rx	lx	sp	
00:00220F 100018              +322 	sx	ONE_TIME_SAVE1
  :                            323

  :                            328

  :                            94 
  :                            95 .	this is the direct feed to udpq from interfaces which have
  :                            96 .	fixed dual-ported buffering. Interfaces with a direct access
  :                            97 .	to system memory would not have this packet copy
  :                            98 
  :                            99 .	The packet copy is used as an occasion to checksum
  :                            100 
  :                            101 .	The packet copy places the packets in rx_buffer with
  :                            102 .	T77 descriptors which are called ACWs
  :                            103 
  :                            104 .	This routine cannot be called for fragmented datagrams
  :                            105 .	because it is instant from frame readout to UDP checksum
  :                            106 .	generation
  :                            107 
  :                            108 .	this is an upward call from ipv4_rx
  :                            109

  :                            137 .	this is a protocol which has a length in the header
  :                            138 .	so the first step is to see if the IP datagram is

  :                            165 	
00:0023C7 B60284              +166 	j	ipv4_rxz
  :                            167 
  :                            168 
  :                            169 .	this is the feed to udpq for reassembled datagrams
  :                            170 .	which are already in a chain of rx_buffer with ACW
  :                            171 .	descriptors
  :                            172 
  :                            173 .	each fragment has the checksum addition of its data
  :                            174 .	in the ACW, to exploit the chance of checksum
  :                            175 .	generation during copy to rx_buffer
  :                            176 
  :                            177 .	it has been second-guessed in all cases that checksum
  :                            178 .	is specified in the UDP header (the UDP header may not
  :                            179 .	be the first fragment of the datagram to arrive).
  :                            180 
  :                            181 .	also, it is unlikely that UDP payload large enough
  :                            182 .	to need reassembly would be exempt from checksum
  :                            183 
  :                            184 .	no-checksum is usually a feature of high-speed
  :                            185 .	media traffic which is transmitted in much smaller
  :                            186 .	packets than any MTU
  :                            187 
  :                            188 .	large reassembly is more likely to be traffic like
  :                            189 .	NFS in 8KByte slabs (or 3KByte slabs where RTA is the
  :                            190 .	NFS client). Checksum would certainly apply
  :                            191 
  :                            192 .	So all the data arriving here has been end-around
  :                            193 .	added and the positive sum left in the ACW
  :                            194 
  :                            195 .	if the UDP header has a nonzero checksum, the data sums
  :                            196 .	are now summed with network address, protocol index
  :                            197 .	and user datagram length, then inverted and if finally
  :                            198 .	zero inverted again. Then compared.
  :                            199 
  :                            200 	$if	RX_FRAGMENTS

  :                            208 	tnz	b		. because zero isn't
  :                            209 	xorB,xi	-1		. a UDP checksum
  :                            210 	xorB	0, sp
  :                            211 	pop	uugram:checksum, x
  :                            212 	la	fp
  :                            213 	jnzb	net_free_chain
  :                            214 udp_rx_rasm_nocheck
  :                            215 	la	fp

  :                            217 	j	net_add_chain
  :                            218 	$endif

  :                            273 
  :                            274 . low level call with the acw adress in X
  :                            275 
  :                            276 . both ports must be in the UDP header
  :                            277 . both adresses must be in the address buffer
  :                            278 
  :                            279 . if there is no come-from address in the address buffer
  :                            280 . the thing cannot now be checksummed and an earlier
  :                            281 . call should have done the internal routing step to
  :                            282 . find the sending interface and its default network
  :                            283 . address
  :                            284 
  :                            285 . so in that case an error gets returned as described
  :                            286 . in the rest of this discussion
  :                            287 
  :                            288 . it's the interface number that is important in
  :                            289 . finding a local address for the datagram and
  :                            290 . for the checksum
  :                            291 
  :                            292 . it's also optimal if the interface index is already
  :                            293 . in the descriptor
  :                            294 
  :                            295 . we have the local address and the interface number
  :                            296 . already when we just bounce back reversing the
  :                            297 . incoming addresses as a server with the calls to
  :                            298 
  :                            299 . 	udp_rtxbuffer->udp_response_buffer
  :                            300 
  :                            301 . and after message construction, to here:
  :                            302 
  :                            303 .	udp_acw_send->udp_txaw
  :                            304 
  :                            305 . some implementations suggest you do a connect
  :                            306 . which makes a routing call to IP and plugs the
  :                            307 . default network address of the interface back
  :                            308 . into the socket
  :                            309 
  :                            310 . having a from address in the socket is
  :                            311 . usable when you are making the conventional
  :                            312 . calls send and sendto
  :                            313 
  :                            314 . the connect idea is nice for send but not
  :                            315 . for sendto which is meant for random desinations
  :                            316 . reached over various interfaces
  :                            317 
  :                            318 . it's also not right that an application should
  :                            319 . need to pick a local network address before
  :                            320 . binding a UDP socket. The application and its
  :                            321 . developer cannot know that unless the
  :                            322 . application is for one host with one interface
  :                            323 
  :                            324 . since this is the final path step of all the
  :                            325 . possible interface calls to send UDP
  :                            326 
  :                            327 . and the step where checksum is done if opted
  :                            328 . and the come-from address therefore needed
  :                            329 
  :                            330 . we'll do the following here
  :                            331 
  :                            332 . we'll return with an error code if there is
  :                            333 . no source address, because
  :                            334 
  :                            335 . if you got here with the primitive
  :                            336 
  :                            337 .	udp_acw_send
  :                            338 
  :                            339 . then it was up to you to copy the address
  :                            340 . information into the buffers. At this
  :                            341 . point it's no longer certain that the
  :                            342 . socket/port structure is based. That's
  :                            343 . not even in the parameters to this call
  :                            344 . and it's intended that the caller decides
  :                            345 . what at this point is based in socket
  :                            346 . space
  :                            347 
  :                            348 . so we'll send back an error
  :                            349 
  :                            350 . if you obtained the buffer with
  :                            351 
  :                            352 .	udp_rtxbuffer
  :                            353 
  :                            354 . then that DID set the addresses up for you
  :                            355 
  :                            356 . if you came from send, send had access
  :                            357 . to your socket and copied the addresses
  :                            358 . in there
  :                            359 
  :                            360 . if you then get to here with no come-from
  :                            361 . address you hadn't done the connect call
  :                            362 . which writes both to and from addresses
  :                            363 . to the socket. So the same error return
  :                            364 . is appropriate, we'll call it something
  :                            365 . like
  :                            366 
  :                            367 . UDP_NOSOURCENET	$equ	-5
  :                            368 
  :                            369 . if you got here from sendto, that makes
  :                            370 . the same internal routing call as connect
  :                            371 . for every datagram
  :                            372 . but writes what it finds to the datagram
  :                            373 . address buffer and not to the socket
  :                            374 
  :                            375 . recvfrom can cheekily write all the
  :                            376 . incoming addressing to the socket
  :                            377 
  :                            378 . then sendto can skip the internal routing
  :                            379 . step if the socket has a source address
  :                            380 . and the destinaton address stored on the
  :                            381 . socket is the sendto parameter address
  :                            382 
  :                            383 . send can just send on the recvfrom
  :                            384 . addresses as if it had connected
  :                            385 
  :                            386 . since the routing call finds the next hop
  :                            387 . as well as the interface, that information
  :                            388 . stays in the address buffer of the datagram
  :                            389 . and IP routing will not need to find the
  :                            390 . next hop again for this one datagram
  :                            391 
  :                            392 . for connect/send the next-hop isn't saved,
  :                            393 . because it might change. But the interface
  :                            394 . is saved
  :                            395 
  :                            396 
  :                            397 
  :                            398 udp_txaw

  :                            662

  :                            666 .	different tasks
  :                            667 
  :                            668 .	udp_write_rxq is called from udp in the IP task
  :                            669 .	the socket structure is in x

  :                            702 .	traffic on loopback will just be copied

  :                            172 
  :                            173 	.	0:	is the remote ACK possible?
  :                            174 
  :                            175 	.	0:5	is there a remote reset?
  :                            176 
  :                            177 	.	1:	cache remote ACK and window
  :                            178 
  :                            179 	.	2:1	send waiting segments as remote
  :                            180 	.		window allows
  :                            181 
  :                            182 	.	2:2	release segments queued for ACK
  :                            183 	.		according to remote ACK value
  :                            184 	.		vs sequence + size of sent output
  :                            185 
  :                            186 	.	3:	compare remote sequence with local
  :                            187 	.		ACK
  :                            188 
  :                            189 	.	4:	if less, shave the leading edge
  :                            190 	.		of the input
  :                            191 
  :                            192 	.	5:	if more but sequence + length
  :                            193 	.		within local ACK + window, deferq
  :                            194 	.		and otherwise drop
  :                            195 
  :                            196 	.	6:	accept as much of the net input at 4:
  :                            197 	.		as local window allowed. This avoids
  :                            198 	.		accepting single bytes which are
  :                            199 	.		sent to push the window open
  :                            200 	.		and also avoids rejecting payload
  :                            201 	.		for which we have advertised
  :                            202 	.		window. Drop the segment if no
  :                            203 	.		part of it is accepted, and shave
  :                            204 	.		the trailing edge if part of it
  :                            205 	.		is rejected
  :                            206 
  :                            207 	.		It might be unwise to accept beyond
  :                            208 	.		window. A well behaved remote system
  :                            209 	.		won't send beyond window (apart from
  :                            210 	.		experimental 1-byters, and the response
  :                            211 	.		to those is drop them and advertise
  :                            212 	.		new window). A malicious remote system
  :                            213 	.		might try to manipulate window
  :                            214 
  :                            215 	.	7:	Advance local ACK and reduce local
  :                            216 	.		window according to the amount accepted
  :                            217 
  :                            218 	.	8:	generate new local window
  :                            219 
  :                            220 	.	9:	transmit segments queued for remote window
  :                            221 	.		according to remote ACK + window,  and
  :                            222 	.		if there is none, and no further input
  :                            223 	.		segment after this has yet been received
  :                            224 	.		for this connection, send an ACK, unless
  :                            225 	.		the incoming segment is an ACK without
  :                            226 	.		payload and its SEQ is not beyond local ACK
  :                            227 
  :                            228 	.		SYN and FIN have a payload weight of
  :                            229 	.		1 byte
  :                            230 
  :                            231 	.		restart connection:timer(1)
  :                            232 	.		maybe do this at an early point, depending
  :                            233 	.		whether the attempt to communicate
  :                            234 	.		counts as keepalive even if it is
  :                            235 	.		out of range
  :                            236 
  :                            237 
  :                            238 
  :                            239 
  :                            240 	.	1	is the remote ACK possible?

  :                            1749 
  :                            1750 .	buffer queues which are shared between tasks have to be accessed
  :                            1751 .	in a protected way. They are mainly the queues which both the IP
  :                            1752 .	task and an application protocol access. Some application protocols
  :                            1753 .	are in the IP task and some are not
  :                            1754 
  :                            1755 .	it would be quite bad to place TS cells in every queue structure
  :                            1756 .	because not all queues need them. Sockets and connections are the
  :                            1757 .	best available place for these lock cells
  :                            1758 
  :                            1759 .	all this code is SMP, so the code
  :                            1760 
  :                            1761 .	1.	prevents itself from getting interrupted or switched
  :                            1762 .	2.	locks the connection
  :                            1763 .	3.	updates the queue
  :                            1764 .	4.	frees the lock
  :                            1765 .	5.	reloads the interrupt mask it had before
  :                            1766

  :                            42 .	these blocking-only calls are central to the
  :                            43 .	known classical POSIX-like socket interface
  :                            44 
  :                            45 .	they correspond to the vectors	listen()
  :                            46 .					accept()
  :                            47 .					close()
  :                            48 .					connect()
  :                            49 
  :                            50 .	their blocking characteristic is in this file connect.msm
  :                            51 
  :                            52 .	and they complement		recv()
  :                            53 .	and				send()
  :                            54 
  :                            55 .	which block or not per flags argument
  :                            56 
  :                            57 .	first parameter of these routines is the handle integer
  :                            58 
  :                            59 .	the target connections of these routines are
  :                            60 .	in the default connections array
  :                            61 .	not the high-volume connection arrays
  :                            62

  :                            247 
  :                            248         $if      QSIMULTANEOUS
  :                            249 
  :                            250 .	these use the i_f index word as a next pointer
  :                            251 .	instead of the next pointer at the front of the
  :                            252 .	descriptor
  :                            253 
  :                            254 .	this is so that TCP segments awaiting ACK
  :                            255 .	can be on the interface queue and the
  :                            256 .	connection's ACK wait queue at the same time
  :                            257 
  :                            258 .	the bytes count in the descriptor has
  :                            259 .	bit 23 on to instruct the interface not
  :                            260 .	to drop the buffer after it is sent
  :                            261 
  :                            262 .	in the case of high volume banks where a
  :                            263 .	collection of TCP connections shares a
  :                            264 .	switched output bank at B80000 for segments
  :                            265 .	awaiting first window then ACK, separate
  :                            266 .	from the fixed transmit buffers at F00000
  :                            267 
  :                            268 .	where the two owners of the buffer are
  :                            269 .	the socket and the interface, it is the
  :                            270 .	interface which uses the next_bdi pointer
  :                            271 .	in the descriptor
  :                            272 
  :                            273 .	when actions are carried out on the socket
  :                            274 .	its transmit buffer bank at B80000 is always
  :                            275 .	based. This is only an issue of high volume
  :                            276 .	transaction connections which are intended to
  :                            277 .	exist in very large numbers and therefore
  :                            278 .	cannot keep all their transmitted segments
  :                            279 .	in the fixed  megaword at F00000 until remote
  :                            280 .	ACK arrives from the network
  :                            281

  :                            468 
  :                            469 .	the method of having signed and unsigned range checks
  :                            470 .	without noticing too much
  :                            471 
  :                            472 .	firstly, if the high-order bit of the upper and lower limit
  :                            473 .	are identical, just do unsigned compares, and that will give
  :                            474 .	the right result whether the candidate integer is meant to
  :                            475 .	be signed or not
  :                            476 
  :                            477 .	secondly, if the high order bits of the two limits are 
  :                            478 .	different, then
  :                            479 
  :                            480 .	if the upper limit interpreted as unsigned is greater than
  :                            481 .	the lower limit interpreted as unsigned (the high order bit
  :                            482 .	of the upper limit is on, and not the high order bit of the
  :                            483 .	lower limit), just do an unsigned compare, because the
  :                            484 .	low-high difference cannot be a negative difference between
  :                            485 .	two signed integers. It can only be a lower limit in the
  :                            486 .	lower half of the number space, and an upper limit in the
  :                            487 .	upper half of the unsigned number space
  :                            488 
  :                            489 .	in all the above cases, the candidate integer is within
  :                            490 .	the range tested, if both carry is generated from the
  :                            491 .	subtraction of the lower limit from the integer, and also
  :                            492 .	carry is generated from the subtraction of the integer from
  :                            493 .	the upper limit
  :                            494 
  :                            495 .	this depends upon carry being consistent with adding the
  :                            496 .	minus of one number to the plus of another
  :                            497 
  :                            498 .	RTA1 is logically correct for this. The result and carried
  :                            499 .	result of
  :                            500 
  :                            501 .		ana	THIS
  :                            502 .		anb	THAT
  :                            503 .		dan	THE_OTHER
  :                            504 
  :                            505 
  :                            506 .	are identical to the result and carried result of
  :                            507 
  :                            508 .		aa	MINUS_THIS
  :                            509 .		ab	MINUS_THAT
  :                            510 .		da	MINUS_THE_OTHER
  :                            511 
  :                            512 .	carry$ set after ana anb dan means the unsigned number in
  :                            513 .	the accumulators was at least as large as the number
  :                            514 .	subtracted from it
  :                            515 .		
  :                            516 .
  :                            517 .	if the high-order bit of the lower limit is on and not the
  :                            518 .	high order bit of the upper limit, this is a signed range
  :                            519 .	which includes the value zero between its upper and lower
  :                            520 .	bounds. Then the candidate integer is within the range
  :                            521 .	tested if both the result of the integer minus the lower limit
  :                            522 .	is of positive polarity, and also the result of the upper limit
  :                            523 .	minus the integer is positive polarity
  :                            524 
  :                            525 .	polarity (and zero) are tested in the registers holding the
  :                            526 .	results, because the only condition flag in RTA1 is carry$
  :                            527 
  :                            528 .	examples of instructions testing results other than carry are
  :                            529 
  :                            530 .		jpa	here
  :                            531 .		jnb	there
  :                            532 .		jdz	everywhere
  :                            533 .		jzb	b_is_zero
  :                            534 .		jpe	a_has_even_parity
  :                            535 
  :                            536 .		tp	location_a_few_million
  :                            537 .		j	the_word_at_location_a_few_million_is_negative
  :                            538 
  :                            539 .		tz	2, sp
  :                            540 .		j	the_third_register_in_the_internal_stack_is_nonzero
  :                            541 
  :                            542 
  :                            543 mib_store_long_range!

00:00A550 5D205A              +123 	ly, xi	a2_ber_transient(3)
00:00A551 3E0719              +124 	lcal	ber_rs
  :                            125 
  :                            126 
  :                            127 
  :                            128 .	the parts to pull out are
  :                            129 .	_________________________
  :                            130 
  :                            131 .	30LL	binding and length		00..7F 81:80..FF 82:0100..FFFF
  :                            132 .	0201vv	SNMP version	00 = v 1
  :                            133 
  :                            134 .	then for v 1 at least...
  :                            135 
  :                            136 .	04LLalpha community string
  :                            137 
  :                            138 .	AxLL	command byte pdu length		00..7F 81:80..FF 82:0100..FFFF
  :                            139 .	160 = get 161 = getnext 163 = set
  :                            140 
  :                            141 .	0204xxxxxxxx	request ID typically 4 bytes
  :                            142 
  :                            143 .	020100	status
  :                            144 .	020100	error index
  :                            145 
  :                            146 .	30LL	binding and length		00..7F 81:80..FF 82:0100..FFFF
  :                            147 .	30LL-2	maybe another one nested
  :                            148 
  :                            149 .	06LLxxxxxx...0500	oids with NULLtype zeroL for get / getnext
  :                            150 .	06LLxxxxxx...4103yyyyyy	oids with typeLL / value for set
  :                            151 
  :                            152 .	strings may be > 127 bytes, length is encoded
  :                            153 .						00..7F 81:80..FF 82:0100..FFFF
  :                            154 
  :                            155 .	______________________________________________________________
  :                            156 
  :                            157 .	if the command is not A0 A1 A3 the request is dropped silently
  :                            158 
  :                            159 .	if the bindings lengths don't figure with the containing lengths
  :                            160 .	the request is dropped silently
  :                            161 
  :                            162 .	if community string is unknown the request is dropped silently
  :                            163 
  :                            164 
  :                            165 .	the response is as the request except
  :                            166 .	_____________________________________
  :                            167 
  :                            168 .	the command byte or response is SNMP_RESPONSE A2 162
  :                            169 
  :                            170 .	the response to successful get / getnext contains
  :                            171 .	a value encoding, not NULLtype/zeroL 0500
  :                            172 
  :                            173 
  :                            174 .	if anything has went wrong
  :                            175 
  :                            176 .		status is the error
  :                            177 
  :                            178 .		1 = something was too large in some way		tooBig
  :                            179 .		2 = that does not exist				noSuchName
  :                            180 .		3 = value wrong type or out of range		badValue
  :                            181 .		4 = object may not be wrote			readOnly
  :                            182 .		5 = something else was wrong			genErr
  :                            183 
  :                            184 .		error index indicates the [1st] oid binding which is in error
  :                            185 .		relative 1, 1 = 1st oid binding in the PDU
  :                            186 
  :                            187 .	the plan is
  :                            188 .	take all the actions and table the results as BER templates
  :                            189 
  :                            190 .	if anything is wrong, send the bindings back as received
  :                            191 .	with error-code set to the mistake
  :                            192 .	and error-index pointing to the first binding which is in error
  :                            193 
  :                            194 .	(no further ones need be scanned if that happens)
  :                            195 .	this means the update values are returned but not actioned
  :                            196 
  :                            197 .	and the read-requests are returned with null values as received
  :                            198 
  :                            199 .	even multiple get-nexts can be sensibly answered on this principle
  :                            200 
  :                            201 .	and if everything has went right, produce the BER-encoded results
  :                            202 
  :                            203
